Avianca case in June 2023 changed legal practice when a federal court sanctioned attorneys who submitted a brief containing fabricated AI-generated case citations. Following this incident, judges and bar associations across the United States issued standing orders, local rules, and guidance addressing AI use in court filings.\n\nAI disclosure requirements vary greatly by jurisdiction, and it is imperative that attorneys remain diligent in identifying whether a specific court or judge requires AI disclosure. The implementation of AI disclosure requirements is an emerging trend in federal and state courts.\n\n### Specific District Examples\n\nSeveral federal districts have adopted AI-related orders:\n\n- In the Northern District of Texas, Judge Brantley Starr requires lawyers to certify whether generative AI was used in preparing filings, and if so, confirm that a human verified all statements and citations\n\n- In the District of New Jersey, Judge Evelyn Padin requires disclosure whenever AI is used, including identification of the specific tool used, description of affected sections, and certification of human review\n\n- In California, 10 federal judges and one state court judge require disclosure and/or certifications of accuracy if AI is used to prepare court documents\n\n### District of Nevada Specifically\n\nUnfortunately, my search did not locate any specific standing orders, general orders, or local rules from the U.S. District Court for the District of Nevada regarding artificial intelligence use during 2024-2026.
